<p>An <b>electric car</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobile</a> that is <a href="/wiki/Ground_propulsion" title="Ground propulsion">propelled</a> by one <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a> or more, using electrical energy stored in <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a> or another energy storage device. <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">Electric motors</a> give electric cars instant torque, creating strong and smooth acceleration.</p>
<p>Electric cars were popular in the late 19th century and early 20th century, until advances in <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> technology and <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a> of cheaper gasoline vehicles led to a decline in the use of <a href="/wiki/Electric_drive_vehicle" title="Electric drive vehicle" class="mw-redirect">electric drive vehicles</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Energy_crises" title="Energy crises" class="mw-redirect">energy crises</a> of the 1970s and 1980s brought a short-lived interest in electric cars; although, those cars did not reach the mass marketing stage, as is the case in the 21st century. Since 2008, a renaissance in electric vehicle manufacturing has occurred due to advances in battery and power management technologies, concerns about increasing <a href="/wiki/2000s_energy_crisis" title="2000s energy crisis">oil prices</a>, and the need to reduce <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions" class="mw-redirect">greenhouse gas emissions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PEVs_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear: left; margin-bottom: .5em; float: left; padding: .5em 1.4em .8em 0; background: none; width: 1;">

<p>As of August 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Series_production" title="Series production" class="mw-redirect">series production</a> highway-capable models available in some countries include the <a href="/wiki/Buddy_(electric_car)" title="Buddy (electric car)">Buddy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_i_MiEV" title="Mitsubishi i MiEV" class="mw-redirect">Mitsubishi i MiEV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chery_QQ3_EV" title="Chery QQ3 EV" class="mw-redirect">Chery QQ3 EV</a>, <a href="/wiki/JAC_J3_EV" title="JAC J3 EV" class="mw-redirect">JAC J3 EV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_ED" title="Smart ED" class="mw-redirect">Smart ED</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheego_Whip_LiFe" title="Wheego Whip LiFe" class="mw-redirect">Wheego Whip LiFe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mia_electric" title="Mia electric">Mia electric</a>, <a href="/wiki/BYD_e6" title="BYD e6">BYD e6</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bollor%C3%A9_Bluecar" title="Bolloré Bluecar">Bolloré Bluecar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renault_Fluence_Z.E." title="Renault Fluence Z.E.">Renault Fluence Z.E.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Focus_Electric" title="Ford Focus Electric">Ford Focus Electric</a>, <a href="/wiki/BMW_ActiveE" title="BMW ActiveE">BMW ActiveE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_S" title="Tesla Model S">Tesla Model S</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honda_Fit_EV" title="Honda Fit EV" class="mw-redirect">Honda Fit EV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota_RAV4_EV_Concept" title="Toyota RAV4 EV Concept" class="mw-redirect">RAV4 EV second generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renault_Zoe" title="Renault Zoe">Renault Zoe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roewe_E50" title="Roewe E50" class="mw-redirect">Roewe E50</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahindra_e2o" title="Mahindra e2o">Mahindra e2o</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lumeneo_Neoma" title="Lumeneo Neoma">Lumeneo Neoma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Spark_EV" title="Chevrolet Spark EV" class="mw-redirect">Chevrolet Spark EV</a>. The world's top-selling highway-capable all-electric cars are the Nissan Leaf, with global sales of over 75,000 units by August 2013;<sup id="cite_ref-Leaf75K_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaf75K-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> the Mitsubishi i-MiEV, with global sales of more than 30,000 vehicles by June 2013, including more than 4,000 <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Minicab_MiEV" title="Mitsubishi Minicab MiEV" class="mw-redirect">minicab MiEVs</a> sold in Japan, and over 10,000 units rebadged as Peugeot iOn and Citroën C-Zero and sold in the European market;<sup id="cite_ref-iMiEV30K_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iMiEV30K-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JapMiEV09_13_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JapMiEV09_13-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Tesla Model S, with 12,700 units delivered through June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Tesla4Q2012_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tesla4Q2012-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tesla1Q2013_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tesla1Q2013-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tesla2Q2013_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tesla2Q2013-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Pure electric car sales in 2012 were led by Japan with a 28% <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> of global sales, followed by the United States with a 26% share, China with 16%, France with 11%, and Norway with 7%.<sup id="cite_ref-Outlook2013_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Outlook2013-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Benefits of electric cars over conventional <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> automobiles include a significant reduction of local <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, as they do not emit <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle_emissions" title="Motor vehicle emissions">tailpipe pollutants</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> in many cases, a large reduction in total <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> and other emissions (dependent on the fuel and technology used for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a><sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PEVs_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup>), and less dependence on foreign oil, which in several countries is cause for concern about vulnerability to oil price volatility and <a href="/wiki/Supply_(economics)" title="Supply (economics)">supply</a> <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">disruption</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reinventing_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reinventing-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PEVs1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs1-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Widespread adoption of electric cars faces several hurdles and limitations, however, including the higher cost of electric vehicles, the lack of <a href="/wiki/Charging_station" title="Charging station">recharging infrastructure</a> (other than home charging) and the driver's fear of the batteries running out of energy before reaching their destination (<a href="/wiki/Range_anxiety" title="Range anxiety">range anxiety</a>) due to the limited range of most existing electric cars.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PEVs_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Electric cars are a variety of <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicle</a> (EV); the term "electric vehicle" refers to any vehicle that uses electric motors for propulsion, while "electric car" generally refers to road-going <a href="/wiki/Automobiles" title="Automobiles" class="mw-redirect">automobiles</a> powered by electricity. While an electric car's power source is not explicitly an on-board battery, electric cars with motors powered by other energy sources are generally referred to by a different name: an electric car powered by sunlight is a <a href="/wiki/Solar_car" title="Solar car">solar car</a>, and an electric car powered by a gasoline generator is a form of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_car" title="Hybrid car" class="mw-redirect">hybrid car</a>. Thus, an electric car that derives its power from an on-board battery pack is a form of <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicle</a> (BEV). Most often, the term "electric car" is used to refer to battery electric vehicles.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_electric_vehicle" title="History of the electric vehicle">History of the electric vehicle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg/220px-1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg/330px-1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg/440px-1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1888_Flocken_Elektrowagen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
German <i>Flocken Elektrowagen</i> of 1888, regarded as the first electric car of the world<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500,_Kraftdroschke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500%2C_Kraftdroschke.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500%2C_Kraftdroschke.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500%2C_Kraftdroschke.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500%2C_Kraftdroschke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500%2C_Kraftdroschke.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500%2C_Kraftdroschke.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-1990-1126-500,_Kraftdroschke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
German electric car, 1904</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png/220px-Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png/330px-Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png/440px-Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Detroit_Electric_car_charging.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Detroit_Electric" title="Detroit Electric">Detroit Electric</a> car charging</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Electric cars enjoyed popularity between the late 19th century and early 20th century, when electricity was among the preferred methods for automobile propulsion, providing a level of comfort and ease of operation that could not be achieved by the gasoline cars of the time. Advances in <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion</a> technology, especially the electric starter, soon rendered this advantage moot; the greater range of gasoline cars, quicker refueling times, and growing petroleum infrastructure, along with the mass production of gasoline vehicles by companies such as the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>, which reduced prices of gasoline cars to less than half that of equivalent electric cars, led to a decline in the use of electric propulsion, effectively removing it from important markets such as the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> by the 1930s. However, in recent years, increased concerns over the <a href="/wiki/Automobile#Environmental_impact" title="Automobile">environmental impact of gasoline cars</a>, higher gasoline prices, improvements in battery technology, and the prospect of <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, have brought about renewed interest in electric cars, which are perceived to be more environmentally friendly and cheaper to maintain and run, despite high initial costs, after a failed reappearance in the late-1990s.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1890s_to_1910s:_Early_history">1890s to 1910s: Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1890s to 1910s: Early history">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1890s to 1910s: Early history" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg/220px-EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg/330px-EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg/440px-EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EdisonElectricCar1913.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Electric" title="Detroit Electric">Detroit Electric</a> car in 1913</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before the pre-eminence of <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engines" title="Internal combustion engines" class="mw-redirect">internal combustion engines</a>, electric automobiles held many speed and distance records. Among the most notable of these records was the breaking of the 100&#160;km/h (62&#160;mph) speed barrier, by <a href="/wiki/Camille_Jenatzy" title="Camille Jenatzy">Camille Jenatzy</a> on April 29, 1899 in his 'rocket-shaped' vehicle <a href="/wiki/Jamais_Contente" title="Jamais Contente" class="mw-redirect">Jamais Contente</a>, which reached a top speed of 106&#160;km/h (66&#160;mph). Before the 1920s, electric automobiles were competing with petroleum-fueled cars for urban use of a quality service car.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Proposed as early as 1896 in order to overcome the lack of recharging infrastructure, an exchangeable battery service was first put into practice by <a href="/wiki/Hartford_Electric_Light_Company" title="Hartford Electric Light Company" class="mw-redirect">Hartford Electric Light Company</a> for electric trucks. The vehicle owner purchased the vehicle from General Electric Company (GVC) without a battery and the electricity was purchased from Hartford Electric through an exchangeable battery. The owner paid a variable per-mile charge and a monthly service fee to cover maintenance and storage of the truck. The service was provided between 1910 to 1924 and during that period covered more than 6 million miles. Beginning in 1917 a similar service was operated in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> for owners of the Milburn Light Electric cars who also could buy the vehicle without the batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-HistEV_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistEV-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/53/1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg/220px-1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg" width="220" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/53/1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg/330px-1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/53/1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg/440px-1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1911_Ideal_Electric_Car_Co_Advertisement.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
1911 Ideal Electric Car ad</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1897, electric vehicles found their first commercial application in the U.S. as a fleet of electrical New York City taxis, built by the Electric Carriage and Wagon Company of Philadelphia. Electric cars were produced in the US by Anthony Electric, <a href="/wiki/Baker_Motor_Vehicle" title="Baker Motor Vehicle">Baker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Automobile_Company" title="Columbia Automobile Company">Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Electric" title="Detroit Electric">Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Parker_Fritchle#Fritchle_Automobile_.26_Battery_Company" title="Oliver Parker Fritchle">Fritchle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Studebaker_Electric_(automobile)" title="Studebaker Electric (automobile)" class="mw-redirect">Studebaker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riker_Electric_Vehicle_Company" title="Riker Electric Vehicle Company">Riker</a>, and others during the early 20th century.</p>
<p>Despite their relatively slow speed, electric vehicles had a number of advantages over their early-1900s competitors. They did not have the vibration, smell, and noise associated with gasoline cars. They did not require gear changes, which for gasoline cars was the most difficult part of driving. Electric cars found popularity among well-heeled customers who used them as <a href="/wiki/City_cars" title="City cars" class="mw-redirect">city cars</a>, where their limited range was less of a disadvantage. The cars were also preferred because they did not require a manual effort to start, as did gasoline cars which featured a hand crank to start the engine. Electric cars were often marketed as suitable vehicles for women drivers due to this ease of operation.</p>
<p>In 1911, the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times" class="mw-redirect">New York Times</a></i> stated that the electric car has long been recognized as "ideal" because it was cleaner, quieter and much more economical than gasoline-powered cars.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Reporting this in 2010, the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Post" title="Washington Post" class="mw-redirect">Washington Post</a></i> commented that "the same unreliability of electric car batteries that flummoxed Thomas Edison persists today."<sup id="cite_ref-wp100425_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wp100425-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1950s_to_1990s:_Pollution_and_energy_problems">1950s to 1990s: Pollution and energy problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1950s to 1990s: Pollution and energy problems">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1950s to 1990s: Pollution and energy problems" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kilowatt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Kilowatt.jpg/220px-Kilowatt.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Kilowatt.jpg/330px-Kilowatt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Kilowatt.jpg/440px-Kilowatt.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kilowatt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Henney_Kilowatt" title="Henney Kilowatt">Henney Kilowatt</a>, a 1961 production electric car based on the <a href="/wiki/Renault_Dauphine" title="Renault Dauphine">Renault Dauphine</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some European nations during World War II experimented with electric cars, but the technology stagnated. Several ventures were established to build electric cars, such as the <a href="/wiki/Henney_Kilowatt" title="Henney Kilowatt">Henney Kilowatt</a>. In 1955, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Air_Pollution_Control_Act" title="Air Pollution Control Act">Air Pollution Control Act</a> helped address the growing emissions problems and this law was later amended to establish regulatory standards for automobiles.<sup id="cite_ref-anderson_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1959, <a href="/wiki/American_Motors" title="American Motors">American Motors Corporation</a> (AMC) and Sonotone Corporation planned a car to be powered by a "self-charging" battery.<sup id="cite_ref-wards2000_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wards2000-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> It was to have <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">sintered</a> plate <a href="/wiki/Nickel-cadmium_battery" title="Nickel-cadmium battery" class="mw-redirect">nickel-cadmium batteries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Nu-Way Industries also showed an experimental electric car with a one-piece plastic body that was to begin production in early-1960.<sup id="cite_ref-wards2000_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wards2000-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Concerns with rapidly decreasing air quality caused by automobiles prompted the U.S. Congress to pass the Electric Vehicle Development Act of 1966 that provided for electric car research by universities and laboratories.<sup id="cite_ref-anderson_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> By the late-1960s, the <a href="/wiki/Big_Three_(automobile_manufacturers)#United_States_and_Canada" title="Big Three (automobile manufacturers)">U.S. and Canada Big Three automakers</a> each had electric car development programs. The much smaller AMC partnered with Gulton Industries to develop a new battery based on <a href="/wiki/Lithium_battery" title="Lithium battery">lithium</a> and use an advanced speed controller.<sup id="cite_ref-Wouk_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wouk-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Although a nickel-cadmium battery was used for an all-electric 1969 <a href="/wiki/Rambler_American" title="Rambler American">Rambler American</a> station wagon, other "plug-in" vehicles were developed with Gulton that included the <a href="/wiki/Amitron" title="Amitron">Amitron</a> and the similar <a href="/wiki/Electron_(vehicle)" title="Electron (vehicle)">Electron</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_crises" title="Energy crises" class="mw-redirect">energy crises</a> of the 1970s and 80s brought about renewed interest in the perceived independence that electric cars had from the fluctuations of the hydrocarbon energy market. In the early 1990s, the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a> (CARB) began a push for more fuel-efficient, lower-emissions vehicles, with the ultimate goal being a move to <a href="/wiki/Zero-emissions_vehicle" title="Zero-emissions vehicle">zero-emissions vehicles</a> such as electric vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> In response, automakers developed electric models, including the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_TEVan" title="Chrysler TEVan">Chrysler TEVan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Ranger_EV" title="Ford Ranger EV">Ford Ranger EV</a> pickup truck, <a href="/wiki/GM_EV1" title="GM EV1" class="mw-redirect">GM EV1</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_S10_EV" title="Chevrolet S10 EV" class="mw-redirect">S10 EV</a> pickup, <a href="/wiki/Honda_EV_Plus" title="Honda EV Plus">Honda EV Plus</a> hatchback, Nissan lithium-battery<a href="/wiki/Nissan_Altra" title="Nissan Altra" class="mw-redirect">Altra EV</a> miniwagon, and <a href="/wiki/Toyota_RAV4_EV" title="Toyota RAV4 EV">Toyota RAV4 EV</a>. These cars were eventually withdrawn from the U.S. market.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s_to_present:_Revival_of_interest">1990s to present: Revival of interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1990s to present: Revival of interest">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1990s to present: Revival of interest" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_electric_vehicle#1990s_to_present:_Revival_of_interest" title="History of the electric vehicle">History of the electric vehicle: Revival of interest</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg/220px-First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg" width="220" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg/330px-First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg/440px-First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Customer_owned_Nissan_Leaf.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
First <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> delivered in the U.S. on the road south of <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" title="Late-2000s recession" class="mw-redirect">global economic recession in the late 2000s</a> led to increased calls for automakers to abandon fuel-inefficient SUVs, which were seen as a symbol of the excess that caused the recession, in favor of small cars, hybrid cars, and electric cars. <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> electric car maker <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a> began development in 2004 on the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a>, which was first delivered to customers in 2008. As of March 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Tesla had sold more than 2,250 Roadsters in at least 31 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-TRSalesMar12_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRSalesMar12-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_i_MiEV" title="Mitsubishi i MiEV" class="mw-redirect">Mitsubishi i MiEV</a> was launched for fleet customers in Japan in July 2009, and for individual customers in April 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AP100331_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP100331-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters100330_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters100330-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> followed by sales to the public in Hong Kong in May 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-HK0510_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HK0510-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> and Australia in July 2010 via leasing.<sup id="cite_ref-AUST10_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AUST10-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Retail customer deliveries of the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> in Japan and the United States began in December 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-JapanDeliver1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JapanDeliver1-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LaunchUS_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaunchUS-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> followed in 2011 by several European countries and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-DeliveriesEU_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeliveriesEU-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CANissan_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CANissan-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg/220px-BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg/330px-BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg/440px-BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BMW_Concept_ActiveE_--_2010_DC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/BMW_ActiveE" title="BMW ActiveE">BMW ActiveE</a> is part of a field testing program aimed to contribute in the development of the <a href="/wiki/BMW_i3" title="BMW i3">BMW i3</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 2011 <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" title="State of the Union address">State of the Union address</a>, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> expressed an ambitious goal of putting 1 million <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-in electric vehicles</a> on the roads in the U.S. by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The objectives include "reducing dependence on oil and ensuring that America leads in the growing electric vehicle manufacturing industry."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Smart_electric_drive" title="Smart electric drive">Smart electric drive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheego_Whip_LiFe" title="Wheego Whip LiFe" class="mw-redirect">Wheego Whip LiFe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mia_electric" title="Mia electric">Mia electric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volvo_C30_Electric" title="Volvo C30 Electric" class="mw-redirect">Volvo C30 Electric</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Focus_Electric" title="Ford Focus Electric">Ford Focus Electric</a> were launched for retail customers during 2011. The <a href="/wiki/BYD_e6" title="BYD e6">BYD e6</a>, released initially for fleet customers in 2010, began retail sales in <a href="/wiki/Shenzhen,_China" title="Shenzhen, China" class="mw-redirect">Shenzhen, China</a> in October, 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bollor%C3%A9_Bluecar" title="Bolloré Bluecar">Bolloré Bluecar</a> was released in December 2011 and deployed for use in the <a href="/wiki/Autolib%27" title="Autolib'">Autolib'</a> <a href="/wiki/Carsharing" title="Carsharing">carsharing</a> service in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-Launch1_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Launch1-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Leasing to individual and corporate customers began in October 2012 and is limited to the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Île-de-France">Île-de-France</a> area.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2011, the Mitsubishi i MiEV became the first electric car to sell more than 10,000 units, including the models badged in Europe as Citroën C-Zero and Peugeot. The record was registered by <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a>. Several months later, the Nissan Leaf overtook the i MiEV as the best selling all-electric car ever.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Models released to the market in 2012 include the <a href="/wiki/BMW_ActiveE" title="BMW ActiveE">BMW ActiveE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coda_(electric_car)" title="Coda (electric car)">Coda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renault_Fluence_Z.E." title="Renault Fluence Z.E.">Renault Fluence Z.E.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_S" title="Tesla Model S">Tesla Model S</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honda_Fit_EV" title="Honda Fit EV" class="mw-redirect">Honda Fit EV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota_RAV4_EV_Concept" title="Toyota RAV4 EV Concept" class="mw-redirect">Toyota RAV4 EV</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renault_Zoe" title="Renault Zoe">Renault Zoe</a>. The Nissan Leaf passed the milestone of 50,000 units sold worldwide in February 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaf50K_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaf50K-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An official announcement from BMW regarding its i3 model that was first previewed in 2011 occurred in late June 2013. The announcement stated that the car would be released onto the retail market in the second quarter of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_internal_combustion_engine_vehicles">Comparison with internal combustion engine vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Comparison with internal combustion engine vehicles">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Comparison with internal combustion engine vehicles" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An important goal for electric vehicles is overcoming the disparity between their costs of development, production, and operation, with respect to those of equivalent <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine vehicles</a> (ICEVs). As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, electric cars are significantly more expensive than conventional <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> vehicles and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">hybrid electric vehicles</a> due to the additional cost of their <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion battery</a> pack.<sup id="cite_ref-NRC09_1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRC09_1-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> However, battery prices are coming down with mass production and are expected to drop further.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Electric cars have several benefits over conventional internal combustion engine automobiles, including a significant reduction of local <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, as they have no tailpipe, and therefore do not emit harmful <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle_emissions" title="Motor vehicle emissions">tailpipe pollutants</a> from the onboard source of power at the point of operation;<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> reduced <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions from the onboard source of power, depending on the fuel and technology used for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a> to charge the batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PEVs_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Electric vehicles generally, compared to gasoline vehicles show significant reductions in overall well-wheel global carbon emissions due to the highly carbon intensive production in mining, pumping, refining, transportation and the efficiencies obtained with gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Electric vehicles provide for less dependence on foreign oil, which for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_energy_independence" title="United States energy independence">United States</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Developed_countries" title="Developed countries" class="mw-redirect">developed</a> or <a href="/wiki/Emerging_countries" title="Emerging countries" class="mw-redirect">emerging countries</a> is cause for concern about vulnerability to oil price volatility and <a href="/wiki/Supply_(economics)" title="Supply (economics)">supply</a> <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">disruption</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reinventing_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reinventing-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PEVs1_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs1-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Also for many <a href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries" class="mw-redirect">developing countries</a>, and particularly for the poorest in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, high oil prices have an adverse impact on their <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_payments" title="Balance of payments">balance of payments</a>, hindering their economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Price">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Price" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg/220px-Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg/330px-Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg/440px-Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mitsubishi_Electric_Car.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Sales of the <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_i_MiEV" title="Mitsubishi i MiEV" class="mw-redirect">Mitsubishi i MiEV</a> to the public began in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in April 2010, in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> in May 2010 and in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> in July 2010.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The up-front purchase price of electric cars is significantly higher than conventional internal combustion engine cars, even after considering <a href="/wiki/Government_incentives_for_plug-in_electric_vehicles" title="Government incentives for plug-in electric vehicles">government incentives for plug-in electric vehicles</a> available in several countries. The primary reason is the high cost of car <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The high purchase price is hindering the mass transition from <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">gasoline cars</a> to electric cars. According to a survey taken by <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_Company" title="Nielsen Company" class="mw-redirect">Nielsen</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a> in 2010, around three quarters of American and British car buyers have or would consider buying an electric car, but they are unwilling to pay more for an electric car. The survey showed that 65% of Americans and 76% of Britons are not willing to pay more for an electric car than the price of a conventional car.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The electric car company <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">laptop battery technology</a> for the battery packs of its electric cars, which are 3 to 4 times cheaper than dedicated electric car battery packs of other auto makers. Dedicated battery packs cost $700–$800 per kilowatt hour, while battery packs using small laptop cells cost about $200. This could drive down the cost of electric cars that use Tesla's battery technology such as the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_RAV4_EV" title="Toyota RAV4 EV">Toyota RAV4 EV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_ED" title="Smart ED" class="mw-redirect">Smart ED</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_X" title="Tesla Model X">Tesla Model X</a> which announced for 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> As of June 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, and based on the three battery size options offered for the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_S" title="Tesla Model S">Tesla Model S</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times" class="mw-redirect">New York Times</a> estimated the cost of automotive battery packs between <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$400</span> to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$500</span> per kilowatt-hour.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A 2013 study by the <a href="/wiki/American_Council_for_an_Energy-Efficient_Economy" title="American Council for an Energy-Efficient Economy">American Council for an Energy-Efficient Economy</a> reported that battery costs came down from <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1,300</span> per kilowatt hour in 2007 to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$500</span> per kilowatt hour in 2012. The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" title="U.S. Department of Energy" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Department of Energy</a> has set cost targets for its sponsored battery research of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$300</span> per kilowatt hour in 2015 and <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$125</span> per kilowatt hour by 2022. Cost reductions through advances in battery technology and higher production volumes will allow plug-in electric vehicles to be more competitive with conventional internal combustion engine vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Several governments have established policies and economic incentives to overcome existing barriers, promote the sales of electric cars, and fund further development of electric vehicles, more cost-effective battery technology and their components. Several national and local governments have established <a href="/wiki/Government_incentives_for_plug-in_electric_vehicles" title="Government incentives for plug-in electric vehicles">tax credits, subsidies, and other incentives</a> to reduce the net purchase price of electric cars and other <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-ins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JAMAFact_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAMAFact-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT0610_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT0610-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GCC0420_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0420-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IRS2009_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRS2009-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maintenance">Maintenance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Maintenance">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Maintenance" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roadster_Goodwood.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Roadster_Goodwood.JPG/220px-Roadster_Goodwood.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Roadster_Goodwood.JPG/330px-Roadster_Goodwood.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Roadster_Goodwood.JPG/440px-Roadster_Goodwood.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roadster_Goodwood.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a>, launched in 2008, has a <a href="/wiki/All-electric_range" title="All-electric range">range</a> of 244&#160;mi (393&#160;km) and ended production in 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Electric cars have expensive batteries that must be replaced but otherwise incur very low maintenance costs, particularly in the case of current lithium-based designs. The documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Killed_the_Electric_Car%3F" title="Who Killed the Electric Car?">Who Killed the Electric Car?</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-wctec_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wctec-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> shows a comparison between the parts that require replacement in gasoline powered cars and EV1s, with the garages stating that they bring the electric cars in every 5,000&#160;mi (8,000&#160;km), rotate the tires, fill the windshield washer fluid and send them back out again.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Running_costs">Running costs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Running costs">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Running costs" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The cost of charging the battery depends on the price paid per kWh of electricity - which varies with location. As of November 2012, a Nissan Leaf driving 500&#160;mi (800&#160;km) per week is estimated to cost <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$600</span> per year in charging costs in <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The EV1 energy use was about 11&#160;kW·h/100&#160;km (0.40&#160;MJ/km; 0.18&#160;kW·h/mi).<sup id="cite_ref-ev1datasheet_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ev1datasheet-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2011/12 <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> uses 21.25&#160;kW·h/100&#160;km (0.765&#160;MJ/km; 0.3420&#160;kW·h/mi) according to the <a href="/wiki/US_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="US Environmental Protection Agency" class="mw-redirect">US Environmental Protection Agency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTEPA_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTEPA-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> These differences reflect the different design and utility targets for the vehicles, and the varying testing standards. The energy use greatly depends on the driving conditions and driving style. Nissan estimates that the Leaf's 5-year operating cost will be <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1,800</span> versus <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$6,000</span> for a gasoline car in the US<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Nissan, the operating cost of the Leaf in the UK is 1.75 <a href="/wiki/Penny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Penny (British decimal coin)">pence</a> per mile (1.09p per km) when charging at an off-peak electricity rate, while a conventional petrol-powered car costs more than 10 pence per mile (6.25p per km). These estimates are based on a national average of British Petrol Economy 7 rates as of January 2012, and assumed 7 hours of charging overnight at the night rate and one hour in the daytime charged at the Tier-2 daytime rate.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The following table compares <a href="/wiki/Out-of-pocket_expenses" title="Out-of-pocket expenses">out-of-pocket</a> fuel costs estimated by the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency according to its official ratings for <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Miles_per_gallon_gasoline_equivalent" title="Miles per gallon gasoline equivalent">miles per gallon gasoline equivalent</a> in the case of <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-in electric vehicles</a>) for series production all-electric passenger vehicles rated by the EPA by May 2013, versus EPA rated most fuel efficient <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-EPAMost_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAMost-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">gasoline-electric hybrid car</a> (<a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius_(XW30)" title="Toyota Prius (XW30)">Toyota Prius third generation</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> and EPA's average new 2013 vehicle, which has a fuel economy of 23&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (10&#160;L/100&#160;km; 28&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-EPAGuide2013_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAGuide2013-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto">
