<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mileage_costs">Mileage costs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mileage costs">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mileage costs" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most of the mileage-related cost of an electric vehicle can be attributed to the maintenance of the battery pack, and its eventual replacement, because an electric vehicle has only around 5 moving parts in its motor, compared to a gasoline car that has hundreds of parts in its <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> To calculate the cost per kilometer of an electric vehicle it is therefore necessary to assign a monetary value to the wear incurred on the battery. With use, the capacity of a battery decreases. However, even an 'end of life' battery which has insufficient capacity has market value as it can be re-purposed, recycled or used as a spare.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The Tesla Roadster's very large battery pack is expected to last seven years with typical driving and costs <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$12,000</span> when pre-purchased today.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Driving 40 miles (64&#160;km) per day for seven years or 102,200 miles (164,500&#160;km) leads to a battery consumption cost of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.1174</span> per 1 mile (1.6&#160;km) or <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$4.70</span> per 40 miles (64&#160;km). The company <a href="/wiki/Better_Place#Economic_model" title="Better Place">Better Place</a> provided another cost comparison as they anticipate meeting contractual obligations to deliver batteries as well as clean electricity to recharge the batteries at a total cost of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.08</span> per 1 mile (1.6&#160;km) in 2010, <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.04</span> per mile by 2015 and <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.02</span> per mile by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-Agassi_TED_talk_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agassi_TED_talk-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> 40 miles (64&#160;km) of driving would initially cost <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$3.20</span> and fall over time to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$0.80</span>.</p>
<p>In 2010 the U.S. government estimated that a battery with a 100 miles (160&#160;km) range would cost about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$33,000</span>. Concerns remain about durability and longevity of the battery.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Total_cost_of_ownership">Total cost of ownership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Total cost of ownership">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Total cost of ownership" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2010 report by <a href="/wiki/J.D._Power_and_Associates" title="J.D. Power and Associates" class="mw-redirect">J.D. Power and Associates</a> states that it is not entirely clear to consumers the <a href="/wiki/Total_cost_of_ownership" title="Total cost of ownership">total cost of ownership</a> of battery electric vehicles over the life of the vehicle, and "<i>there is still much confusion about how long one would have to own such a vehicle to realize cost savings on fuel, compared with a vehicle powered by a conventional internal combustion engine (ICE). The resale value of HEVs and BEVs, as well as the cost of replacing depleted battery packs, are other financial considerations that weigh heavily on consumers’ minds</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A study published in 2011 by the <a href="/wiki/Belfer_Center_for_Science_and_International_Affairs" title="Belfer Center for Science and International Affairs">Belfer Center</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, found that the gasoline costs savings of <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_car" title="Plug-in electric car" class="mw-redirect">plug-in electric cars</a> over their lifetimes do not offset their higher purchase prices. The study compared the lifetime <a href="/wiki/Net_present_value" title="Net present value">net present value</a> at 2010 purchase and operating costs for the US market with no <a href="/wiki/Government_incentives_for_plug-in_electric_vehicles#United_States" title="Government incentives for plug-in electric vehicles">government subidies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KSG11_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSG11-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KSGpdf_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSGpdf-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> The study estimated that a <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">PHEV</a>-40 is <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$5,377</span> more expensive than a conventional internal combustion engine, while a battery electric vehicle is <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$4,819</span> more expensive. But assuming that battery costs will decrease and gasoline prices increase over the next 10 to 20 years, the study found that BEVs will be significantly cheaper than conventional cars (<span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1,155</span> to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$7,181</span> cheaper). PHEVs, will be more expensive than BEVs in almost all comparison scenarios, and more expensive than conventional cars unless battery costs are very low and gasoline prices high. Savings differ because BEVs are simpler to build and do not use liquid fuel, while PHEVs have more complicated power trains and still have gasoline-powered engines.<sup id="cite_ref-KSG11_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KSG11-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_and_recharging_time">Range and recharging time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Range and recharging time">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Range and recharging time" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most cars with internal combustion engines can be considered to have indefinite range, as they can be refueled very quickly. Electric cars often have less maximum range on one charge than cars powered by fossil fuels, and they can take considerable time to recharge. This is a reason that many automakers marketed EVs as "daily drivers" suitable for city trips and other short hauls.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The average American drives less than 40 miles (64&#160;km) per day; so the <a href="/wiki/GM_EV1" title="GM EV1" class="mw-redirect">GM EV1</a> would have been adequate for the daily driving needs of about 90% of U.S. consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-wctec_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wctec-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, people can be concerned that they would run out of energy from their battery before reaching their destination, a worry known as <a href="/wiki/Range_anxiety" title="Range anxiety">range anxiety</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a> can travel 245 miles (394&#160;km) per charge;<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> more than double that of prototypes and <a href="/wiki/Evaluation" title="Evaluation">evaluation</a> <a href="/wiki/Fleet_vehicle" title="Fleet vehicle">fleet cars</a> currently on the roads.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> The Roadster can be fully recharged in about 3.5 hours from a 220-volt, 70-<a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">amp</a> outlet which can be installed in a home.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> But using a European standard 220-volt, 16-<a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">amp</a> outlet a full charge will take more than 15 hours.</p>
<p>However, most vehicles also support much faster charging, where a suitable power supply is available. Therefore for long distance travel, in the US and elsewhere, there has been the installation of <a href="/wiki/Battery_exchange_station#Fast_charging" title="Battery exchange station" class="mw-redirect">DC Fast Charging</a> stations with high-speed charging capability from <a href="/wiki/Three-phase_electric_power" title="Three-phase electric power">three-phase</a> industrial outlets so that consumers could recharge the 100-200+ mile battery of their electric vehicle to 80 percent in about 30 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> A nationwide fast charging infrastructure is currently being deployed in the US that by 2013 will cover the entire nation.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> DC Fast Chargers are going to be installed at 45 <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a> and <a href="/wiki/ARCO" title="ARCO">ARCO</a> locations and will be made available to the public as early as March 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> The EV Project will deploy charge infrastructure in 16 cities and major metropolitan areas in six states.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Nissan has announced that 200 of its dealers in Japan will install fast chargers for the December 2010 launch of its <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Leaf</a> EV, with the goal of having fast chargers everywhere in Japan within a 25 mile radius.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Although charging at these stations is still relatively time consuming compared to refueling, in practice it often meshes well with a normal driving pattern, where driving is usually done for a few hours before stopping and resting and drink or eating; this gives the car a chance to be charged.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Another way to extend the limited range of electric vehicles is by building them with <a href="/wiki/Battery_swapping" title="Battery swapping" class="mw-redirect">battery switch technology</a>. An EV built with such technology can be able to go to a battery switch station and swap a depleted battery with a fully charged one in a few minutes. In 2011 <a href="/wiki/Better_Place" title="Better Place">Better Place</a> deployed the first modern commercial application of the battery switching model, but due to financial difficulties, the company filed for bankruptcy in May 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-BP01_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP01-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BP02_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP02-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BP03_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP03-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A similar idea is that of the range-extension trailer which is attached only when going on long trips. The trailers can either be owned or rented only when necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/BMW_i" title="BMW i">BMW i</a> will offer a gasoline-powered <a href="/wiki/Range-extended_vehicle" title="Range-extended vehicle" class="mw-redirect">range extender</a> engine as an option for its upcoming <a href="/wiki/BMW_i3" title="BMW i3">BMW i3</a> all-electric car.<sup id="cite_ref-MA0710_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MA0710-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> The company is also planning to offer additional mobility packages for trips where the range of an BMW i3 would not be enough to allow customers to cover longer distances, by providing a conventional BMW vehicle on a given number of days per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Geneva2013_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geneva2013-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> The i3 performance in range-extending mode may be more limited than when it is running on battery power, as BMW clarified that the range extender is designed not for long-distance travel but purely as an emergency backup to keep the electric system going until the next recharging location.<sup id="cite_ref-GCR032013_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCR032013-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> The range-extender option will cost an additional <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$3,850</span> in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT072013_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT072013-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> an additional €4,710 (~ <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$6,300</span>) in France,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> and €4,490 (~ <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$6,000</span>) in the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution_and_carbon_emissions">Air pollution and carbon emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Air pollution and carbon emissions">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Air pollution and carbon emissions" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle#Air_pollution_and_greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">Greenhouse gas emissions in plug-in electric vehicles</a>&#160;and <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid#Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Plug-in hybrid">Greenhouse gas emissions in plug-in hybrids</a></div>
<p>Electric cars contribute to cleaner air in cities because they produce no harmful <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle_emissions" title="Motor vehicle emissions">pollution at the tailpipe</a> from the onboard source of power, such as <a href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate" class="mw-redirect">particulates</a> (<a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a>), <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and various <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">oxides of nitrogen</a>. The clean air benefit is usually local because, depending on the source of the electricity used to recharge the batteries, air pollutant emissions are shifted to the location of the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generation plants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> The amount of carbon dioxide emitted depends on the <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a> of the power source used to charge the vehicle, the efficiency of the said vehicle and the energy wasted in the charging process. This is referred to as <a href="/wiki/The_long_tailpipe" title="The long tailpipe">the long tailpipe</a> of electric vehicles.</p>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Mains_electricity" title="Mains electricity">mains electricity</a> the emission intensity varies significantly per country and within a particular country it will vary depending on demand,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> the availability of renewable sources and the efficiency of the fossil fuel-based generation used at a given time.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Charging a vehicle using renewable energy yields very low carbon footprint (only that to produce and install the generation system e.g. <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>).</p>
<dl>
<dt>United States</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png/220px-2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png/330px-2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png/440px-2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2008_US_electricity_generation_by_source_v2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Sources of electricity in the U.S. in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Eia.doe.gov_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eia.doe.gov-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An EV recharged from the US grid electricity in 2008 emits about 115&#160;grams of <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
2</span></span> per kilometer driven (6.5&#160;oz(<span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
2</span></span>)/mi), whereas a conventional US-market gasoline powered car emits 250&#160;g(CO<sub>2</sub>)/km (14&#160;oz(CO<sub>2</sub>)/mi) (most from its tailpipe, some from the production and distribution of gasoline).<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Concerned_Scientists" title="Union of Concerned Scientists">Union of Concerned Scientists</a> (UCS) published in 2012 a report with an assessment of average greenhouse gas emissions resulting from charging plug-in car batteries considering the full life-cycle (<a href="/wiki/Well-to-wheel" title="Well-to-wheel" class="mw-redirect">well-to-wheel</a> analysis) and according to fuel and technology used to generate electric power by region in the U.S. The study used the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> all-electric car to establish the analysis's baseline. The UCS study expressed the results in terms of <a href="/wiki/Miles_per_gallon" title="Miles per gallon" class="mw-redirect">miles per gallon</a> instead of the conventional unit of grams of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide</a> emissions per year. The study found that in areas where electricity is generated from natural gas, nuclear, hydroelectric or other renewable sources, the potential of plug-in electric cars to reduce greenhouse emissions is significant. On the other hand, in regions where a high proportion of power is generated from coal, <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_car" title="Hybrid electric car" class="mw-redirect">hybrid electric cars</a> produce less CO<sub>2</sub> emissions than <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_car" title="Plug-in electric car" class="mw-redirect">plug-in electric cars</a>, and the best fuel efficient gasoline-powered <a href="/wiki/Subcompact_car" title="Subcompact car">subcompact car</a> produces slightly less emissions than a plug-in car. In the worst-case scenario, the study estimated that for a region where all energy is generated from coal, a plug-in electric car would emit greenhouse gas emissions equivalent to a gasoline car rated at a combined city/highway fuel economy of 30&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (7.8&#160;L/100&#160;km; 36&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>). In contrast, in a region that is completely reliant on natural gas, the plug-in would be equivalent to a gasoline-powered car rated at 50&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (4.7&#160;L/100&#160;km; 60&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>) combined.<sup id="cite_ref-UCS2012EVs_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCS2012EVs-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTCO201_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTCO201-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The study found that for 45% of the U.S. population, a plug-in electric car will generate lower CO<sub>2</sub> emissions than a gasoline-powered car capable of a combined fuel economy of 50&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (4.7&#160;L/100&#160;km; 60&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>), such as the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius_(XW30)" title="Toyota Prius (XW30)">Toyota Prius</a>. Cities in this group included <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland, Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a>, and the cleanest cities achieved well-to-wheel emissions equivalent to a fuel economy of 79&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (3.0&#160;L/100&#160;km; 95&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>). The study also found that for 37% of the population, the electric car emissions will fall in the range of a gasoline-powered car rated at a combined fuel economy between 41 to 50 mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (5.7 to 4.7 L/100&#160;km; 49 to 60 mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>), such as the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_Hybrid#Third_generation_.282011-_.29" title="Honda Civic Hybrid">Honda Civic Hybrid</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lexus_CT200h" title="Lexus CT200h" class="mw-redirect">Lexus CT200h</a>. Cities in this group include <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus, Ohio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" title="Atlanta, Georgia" class="mw-redirect">Atlanta, Georgia</a>. An 18% of the population lives in areas where the power supply is more dependent on burning carbon, and emissions will be equivalent to a car rated at a combined fuel economy between 31 to 40 mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (7.6 to 5.9 L/100&#160;km; 37 to 48 mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>), such as the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Cruze#2008.E2.80.93present_.28J300.29" title="Chevrolet Cruze">Chevrolet Cruze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ford_Focus_(third_generation)" title="Ford Focus (third generation)">Ford Focus</a>. This group includes <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis,_Missouri" title="Saint Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">Saint Louis, Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City" title="Oklahoma City">Oklahoma City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTCO201_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTCO201-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTCO202_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTCO202-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTCO203_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTCO203-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> The study found that there are no regions in the U.S. where plug-in electric cars will have higher greenhouse gas emissions than the average new compact gasoline engine automobile, and the area with the dirtiest power supply produces CO<sub>2</sub> emissions equivalent to a gasoline-powered car rated 33&#160;mpg<sub><small>-US</small></sub> (7.1&#160;L/100&#160;km; 40&#160;mpg<sub><small>-imp</small></sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-UCS2012EVs_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCS2012EVs-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The following table compares well-to-wheels greenhouse gas emissions estimated by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="U.S. Environmental Protection Agency" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Environmental Protection Agency</a> for series production <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_car" title="Plug-in electric car" class="mw-redirect">plug-in electric cars</a> from major carmakers available in the U.S. market by April 2012. For comparison purposes, emissions for the average gasoline-powered new car are also included. Total emissions include the emissions associated with the production, transmission and distribution of electricity used to charge the vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EPAGHGtool_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAGHGtool-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<dl>
<dt>United Kingdom</dt>
</dl>
<p>A study made in the <a href="/wiki/UK" title="UK" class="mw-redirect">UK</a> in 2008 concluded that electric vehicles had the potential to cut down <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions by at least 40%, even taking into account the emissions due to current electricity generation in the UK and emissions relating to the production and disposal of electric vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The savings are questionable relative to hybrid or diesel cars (according to official British government testing, the most efficient European market cars are well below 115&#160;grams of <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
2</span></span> per kilometer driven, although a study in Scotland gave 149.5g<span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
2</span></span>/km as the average for new cars in the UK<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup>), but since UK consumers can select their energy suppliers, it also will depend on how 'green' their chosen supplier is in providing energy into the grid.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Germany</dt>
</dl>
<p>In a worst-case scenario where incremental electricity demand would be met exclusively with coal, a 2009 study conducted by the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a> and IZES found that a mid-size EV would emit roughly 200&#160;g(CO<sub>2</sub>)/km (11&#160;oz(CO<sub>2</sub>)/mi), compared with an average of 170&#160;g(CO<sub>2</sub>)/km (9.7&#160;oz(CO<sub>2</sub>)/mi) for a gasoline-powered compact car.<sup id="cite_ref-Palm010509_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palm010509-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> This study concluded that introducing 1&#160;million EV cars to Germany would, in the best-case scenario, only reduce <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
2</span></span> emissions by 0.1%, if nothing is done to upgrade the electricity infrastructure or manage demand.<sup id="cite_ref-Palm010509_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palm010509-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>France</dt>
</dl>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, which has a clean energy grid, <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
2</span></span> emissions from electric car use would be about 12g per kilometer.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Emissions during production</dt>
</dl>
<p>A 2011 report prepared by <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_plc" title="Ricardo plc">Ricardo</a> found that <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">hybrid electric vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrids</a> and all-electric cars generate more carbon emissions during their production than current conventional vehicles, but still have a lower overall <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" title="Life cycle assessment" class="mw-redirect">full life cycle</a>. The initial higher carbon footprint is due mainly to battery production. As an example, the study estimated that 43 percent of production emissions for a <a href="/wiki/Mid-size_car" title="Mid-size car">mid-size</a> electric car are generated from the battery production.<sup id="cite_ref-Ricardo2011_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ricardo2011-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acceleration_and_drivetrain_design">Acceleration and drivetrain design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Acceleration and drivetrain design">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Acceleration and drivetrain design" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Electric motors can provide high <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight_ratio" title="Power-to-weight ratio">power-to-weight ratios</a>, and batteries can be designed to supply the large currents to support these motors.</p>
<p>Although some electric vehicles have very small motors, 15&#160;kW (20&#160;hp) or less and therefore have modest acceleration, many electric cars have large motors and brisk acceleration. In addition, the relatively constant torque of an electric motor, even at very low speeds tends to increase the acceleration performance of an electric vehicle relative to that of the same rated <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">motor power</a> internal combustion engine. Another early solution was <a href="/wiki/American_Motors" title="American Motors">American Motors</a>’ experimental <a href="/wiki/Amitron" title="Amitron">Amitron</a> piggyback system of batteries with one type designed for sustained speeds while a different set boosted acceleration when needed.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Electric vehicles can also use a direct motor-to-wheel configuration which increases the amount of available <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a>. Having multiple motors connected directly to the wheels allows for each of the wheels to be used for both propulsion and as braking systems, thereby increasing <a href="/wiki/Traction_(engineering)" title="Traction (engineering)">traction</a>. In some cases, the motor can be housed directly in the wheel, such as in the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Whispering_Wheel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Whispering Wheel (page does not exist)">Whispering Wheel</a> design, which lowers the vehicle's <a href="/wiki/Center_of_gravity" title="Center of gravity" class="mw-redirect">center of gravity</a> and reduces the number of moving parts.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> When not fitted with an <a href="/wiki/Axle" title="Axle">axle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Differential_(mechanics)" title="Differential (mechanics)" class="mw-redirect">differential</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(mechanics)" title="Transmission (mechanics)">transmission</a>, electric vehicles have less drivetrain rotational inertia. Housing the motor within the wheel can increase the <a href="/wiki/Unsprung_weight" title="Unsprung weight" class="mw-redirect">unsprung weight</a> of the wheel, which can have an adverse effect on the handling of the vehicle.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Transmission">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Transmission" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A gearless or single gear design in some EVs eliminates the need for gear shifting, giving such vehicles both smoother acceleration and smoother braking. Because the torque of an electric motor is a function of current, not rotational speed, electric vehicles have a high torque over a larger range of speeds during acceleration, as compared to an internal combustion engine. As there is no delay in developing torque in an EV, EV drivers report generally high satisfaction with acceleration.</p>
<p>The gearless design is the least complex, but high acceleration requires high torque from the motor, which requires high current and results in <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">Joule heating</a>. This is because the internal wiring of the motor has electrical resistance, which dissipates power as heat when a current is put through it, in accordance to <a href="/wiki/Ohm%27s_Law" title="Ohm's Law" class="mw-redirect">Ohm's Law</a>. While the torque of the electric motor is not dependent on its rotational speed, the output <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a> of the motor is the product of both the torque and the rotational speed, which means that more power is lost in proportion to the output power when the motor is turning slowly. In effect, the drive train becomes less efficient the slower the vehicle moves.</p>
<p>In the single gear design, this problem is mitigated by using a gear ratio that allows the motor to turn faster than the wheel, which translates low torque and high rotational speed of the motor into high torque and low rotational speed of the wheels, giving equal or better acceleration without compromising efficiency as much. However, since the motor does have a top speed at which it can operate, the trade-off is lower top speed for the vehicle. If a higher top speed is desired, the trade-off is lower acceleration and lower efficiency at slow speeds.</p>
<p>The use of a multiple-speed transmission allows the vehicle to operate efficiently at both high and low speeds, but comes with more complexity and cost.</p>
<p>For example, the <a href="/wiki/Venturi_Fetish" title="Venturi Fetish" class="mw-redirect">Venturi Fetish</a> delivers <a href="/wiki/Supercar" title="Supercar">supercar</a> acceleration despite a relatively modest 220&#160;kW (295&#160;hp), and top speed of around 160&#160;km/h (100&#160;mph). Some <a href="/wiki/Direct_current_motor" title="Direct current motor" class="mw-redirect">DC motor</a>-equipped drag racer EVs, have simple two-speed <a href="/wiki/Manual_transmission" title="Manual transmission">manual transmissions</a> to improve top speed.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a> 2.5 Sport can accelerate from 0 to 97 km/h (0 to 60 mph) in 3.7&#160;seconds with a motor rated at 215&#160;kW (288&#160;hp).<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> The Tesla Model S Performance currently holds the world record for the quickest production electric car to do 402&#160;m (<span style="white-space:nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>&#160;mi), which it did in 12.37&#160;seconds at 178.3&#160;km/h (110.8&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> And the <a href="/wiki/Wrightspeed_X1" title="Wrightspeed X1">Wrightspeed X1</a> prototype created by <a href="/wiki/Wrightspeed_X1" title="Wrightspeed X1">Wrightspeed Inc</a> is the worlds fastest <a href="/wiki/Street-legal_vehicle" title="Street-legal vehicle">street legal</a> electric car to accelerate from 0 to 97 km/h (0 to 60 mph), which it does in 2.9&#160;seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_efficiency">Energy efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Energy efficiency">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Energy efficiency" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">Fuel efficiency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrical_efficiency" title="Electrical efficiency">Electrical efficiency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">Thermal efficiency</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">Energy conversion efficiency</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">Internal combustion engines</a> are relatively inefficient at converting on-board fuel energy to propulsion as most of the energy is wasted as heat. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motors</a> are more <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">efficient</a> in converting stored energy into driving a vehicle, and <a href="/wiki/Electric_drive_vehicle" title="Electric drive vehicle" class="mw-redirect">electric drive vehicles</a> do not consume energy while at rest or coasting, and some of the energy lost when braking is captured and reused through <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking" class="mw-redirect">regenerative braking</a>, which captures as much as one fifth of the energy normally lost during braking.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PEVs2_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs2-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> Typically, conventional <a href="/wiki/Gasoline_engine" title="Gasoline engine" class="mw-redirect">gasoline engines</a> effectively use only 15% of the fuel energy content to move the vehicle or to power accessories, and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engines</a> can reach on-board efficiencies of 20%, while electric drive vehicles have on-board efficiency of around 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-PEVs2_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEVs2-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Production and <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_conversion" title="Electric vehicle conversion">conversion</a> electric cars typically use 10 to 23&#160;kW·h/100&#160;km (0.17 to 0.37&#160;kW·h/mi).<sup id="cite_ref-ev1datasheet_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ev1datasheet-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Approximately 20% of this power consumption is due to <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">inefficiencies</a> in charging the batteries. Tesla Motors indicates that the vehicle efficiency (including charging inefficiencies) of their <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion battery</a> powered vehicle is 12.7&#160;kW·h/100&#160;km (0.21&#160;kW·h/mi) and the well-to-wheels efficiency (assuming the electricity is generated from natural gas) is 24.4&#160;kW·h/100&#160;km (0.39&#160;kW·h/mi).<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabin_heating_and_cooling">Cabin heating and cooling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cabin heating and cooling">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cabin heating and cooling" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Electric vehicles generate very little waste heat and resistance electric heat may have to be used to heat the interior of the vehicle if heat generated from battery charging/discharging can not be used to heat the interior.</p>
<p>While heating can be simply provided with an electric resistance heater, higher efficiency and integral cooling can be obtained with a reversible <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">heat pump</a> (this is currently implemented in the hybrid <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a>). <a href="/wiki/Positive_Temperature_Coefficient" title="Positive Temperature Coefficient" class="mw-redirect">Positive Temperature Coefficient</a> (PTC) junction cooling<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> is also attractive for its simplicity&#160;— this kind of system is used for example in the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a>.</p>
<p>Because electric cars' cabin climate control system does not depend on an internal combustion engine running, to avoid impacting the electric car range some models allow the cabin to be already at the correct temperature at the time the car is next to be used. For example, the Nissan Leaf and the Mistubishi i-MiEV can be pre-heated when the vehicle is plugged in to reduce the impact on range due to cabin heating.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some electric cars, for example the <a href="/wiki/Citro%C3%ABn_Berlingo_Electrique" title="Citroën Berlingo Electrique" class="mw-redirect">Citroën Berlingo Electrique</a>, use an auxiliary heating system (for example <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>-fueled units manufactured by Webasto or Eberspächer) but sacrifice "green" and "Zero emissions" credentials. Cabin cooling can be augmented with <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, most simply and effectively by inducting outside air to avoid extreme heat buildup when the vehicle is closed and parked in the sunlight (such cooling mechanisms are available as <a href="/wiki/Aftermarket_(automotive)" title="Aftermarket (automotive)">aftermarket</a> kits for conventional vehicles). Two models of the 2010 Toyota Prius include this feature as an option.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Safety">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Safety" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The safety issues of BEVs are largely dealt with by the international standard <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a> 6469. This document is divided in three parts dealing with specific issues:</p>
<ul>
<li>On-board electrical energy storage, i.e. the battery</li>
<li>Functional safety means and protection against failures</li>
<li>Protection of persons against electrical hazards.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_of_fire">Risk of fire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Risk of fire">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Risk of fire" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle_fire_incidents" title="Plug-in electric vehicle fire incidents">Plug-in electric vehicle fire incidents</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG/220px-Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG/330px-Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG/440px-Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Volvo_C30_Electric_WAS_2011_852.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Frontal crash test of a <a href="/wiki/Volvo_C30#C30_DRIVe_Electric" title="Volvo C30">Volvo C30 DRIVe Electric</a> to assess the safety of the battery pack.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">Lithium-ion batteries</a> may suffer <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">thermal runaway</a> and cell rupture if overheated or overcharged, and in extreme cases this can lead to combustion.<sup id="cite_ref-Spotnitz:2003a_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spotnitz:2003a-155"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle_fire_incidents" title="Plug-in electric vehicle fire incidents">plug-in electric vehicle fire incidents</a> have taken place since the introduction of mass-production <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-in electric vehicles</a> in 2010. Most of them have been thermal runaway incidents related to their lithium-ion battery packs, and have involved the <a href="/wiki/Zotye_Auto" title="Zotye Auto">Zotye M300 EV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fisker_Karma" title="Fisker Karma">Fisker Karma</a>, <a href="/wiki/BYD_e6" title="BYD e6">BYD e6</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dodge_Ram_1500_Plug-in_Hybrid" title="Dodge Ram 1500 Plug-in Hybrid" class="mw-redirect">Dodge Ram 1500 Plug-in Hybrid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid" title="Toyota Prius Plug-in Hybrid">Toyota Prius Plug-in Hybrid</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_i-MiEV" title="Mitsubishi i-MiEV">Mitsubishi i-MiEV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Outlander_P-HEV" title="Mitsubishi Outlander P-HEV" class="mw-redirect">Outlander P-HEV</a>. As of June 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, no fires after a crash have been reported in the U.S. associated with any of the plug-in electric cars available in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> The only crash related fire reported took place in China in May 2012, after a high-speed car crashed into a <a href="/wiki/BYD_e6" title="BYD e6">BYD e6</a> taxi in <a href="/wiki/Shenzhen" title="Shenzhen">Shenzhen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BYDe6Fire_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BYDe6Fire-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, General Motors ran in several cities a training program for <a href="/wiki/Firefighter" title="Firefighter">firefighters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Certified_first_responder" title="Certified first responder">first responders</a> to demonstrate the sequence of tasks required to safely disable the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a>’s powertrain and its 12 volt electrical system, which controls its high-voltage components, and then proceed to extricate injured occupants. The Volt's high-voltage system is designed to shut down automatically in the event of an airbag deployment, and to detect a loss of communication from an airbag control module.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> GM also made available an Emergency Response Guide for the 2011 Volt for use by emergency responders. The guide also describes methods of disabling the high voltage system and identifies cut zone information.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Nissan also published a guide for first responders that details procedures for handling a damaged 2011 Leaf at the scene of an accident, including a manual high-voltage system shutdown, rather than the automatic process built-in the car's safety systems.<sup id="cite_ref-LeafCrash_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeafCrash-161"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vehicle_safety">Vehicle safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Vehicle safety">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Vehicle safety" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Great effort is taken to keep the mass of an electric vehicle as low as possible to improve its range and endurance. However, the weight and bulk of the batteries themselves usually makes an EV heavier than a comparable gasoline vehicle, reducing range and leading to longer braking distances; it also has less interior space. However, in a collision, the occupants of a heavy vehicle will, on average, suffer fewer and less serious injuries than the occupants of a lighter vehicle; therefore, the additional weight brings safety benefits<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> despite having a negative effect on the car's performance.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> An accident in a 2,000&#160;lb (900&#160;kg) vehicle will on average cause about 50% more injuries to its occupants than a 3,000&#160;lb (1,400&#160;kg) vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> In a single car accident,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and for the other car in a two car accident, the increased mass causes an increase in accelerations and hence an increase in the severity of the accident. Some electric cars use <a href="/wiki/Low_rolling_resistance_tires" title="Low rolling resistance tires" class="mw-redirect">low rolling resistance tires</a>, which typically offer less grip than normal tires.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Many electric cars have a small, light and fragile body, though, and therefore offer inadequate safety protection. The <a href="/wiki/Insurance_Institute_for_Highway_Safety" title="Insurance Institute for Highway Safety">Insurance Institute for Highway Safety</a> in America had condemned the use of <a href="/wiki/Low-speed_vehicle" title="Low-speed vehicle">low speed vehicles</a> and "mini trucks," referred to as <a href="/wiki/Neighborhood_Electric_Vehicle" title="Neighborhood Electric Vehicle">neighborhood electric vehicles</a> (NEVs) when powered by electric motors, on public roads.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazard_to_pedestrians">Hazard to pedestrians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hazard to pedestrians">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hazard to pedestrians" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_warning_sounds" title="Electric vehicle warning sounds">Electric vehicle warning sounds</a></div>
<p>At low speeds, electric cars produced less <a href="/wiki/Roadway_noise" title="Roadway noise">roadway noise</a> as compared to vehicles propelled by <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>. Blind people or the <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">visually impaired</a> consider the noise of combustion engines a helpful aid while crossing streets, hence electric cars and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrids</a> could pose an unexpected hazard.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Economist0509_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist0509-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> Tests have shown that this is a valid concern, as vehicles operating in electric mode can be particularly hard to hear below 20&#160;mph (30&#160;km/h) for all types of road users and not only the visually impaired. At higher speeds, the sound created by tire friction and the air displaced by the vehicle start to make sufficient audible noise.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist0509_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist0509-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Government of Japan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Congress</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> passed legislation to regulate the minimum level of sound for hybrids and <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-in electric vehicles</a> when operating in electric mode, so that blind people and other pedestrians and cyclists can hear them coming and detect from which direction they are approaching.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist0509_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist0509-171"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TMC0810_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TMC0810-173"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> was the first electric car to use Nissan's <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Sound_for_Pedestrians" title="Vehicle Sound for Pedestrians" class="mw-redirect">Vehicle Sound for Pedestrians</a> system, which includes one sound for forward motion and another for reverse.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT0610_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT0610-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTRingT_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTRingT-175"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> As of March 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, most of the hybrids and plug-in electric cars available in the United States make warning noises using a speaker system. The <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_S" title="Tesla Model S">Tesla Model S</a> is one of the few electric-drive cars without warning sounds, because <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a> is awaiting the <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">National Highway Traffic Safety Administration</a> final rule.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNews0313_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoNews0313-176"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differences_in_controls">Differences in controls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Differences in controls">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Differences in controls" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Presently most EV manufacturers do their best to emulate the driving experience as closely as possible to that of a car with a conventional <a href="/wiki/Automatic_transmission" title="Automatic transmission">automatic transmission</a> that motorists are familiar with. Most models therefore have a PRNDL selector traditionally found in cars with automatic transmission despite the underlying mechanical differences. Push buttons are the easiest to implement as all modes are implemented through software on the vehicle's controller.</p>
<p>Even though the motor may be permanently connected to the wheels through a fixed-ratio gear and no <a href="/wiki/Parking_pawl" title="Parking pawl">parking pawl</a> may be present the modes "P" and "N" will still be provided on the selector. In this case the motor is disabled in "N" and an electrically actuated <a href="/wiki/Hand_brake" title="Hand brake" class="mw-redirect">hand brake</a> provides the "P" mode.</p>
<p>In some cars the motor will spin slowly to provide a small amount of creep in "D", similar to a traditional automatic.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When the foot is lifted from the accelerator of an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">ICE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engine_braking" title="Engine braking">engine braking</a> causes the car to slow. An EV would coast under these conditions, and applying mild regenerative braking instead provides a more familiar response. Selecting the L mode will increase this effect for sustained downhill driving, analogous to selecting a lower gear.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Batteries">Batteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Batteries">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Batteries" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_Lithium_Ion_Polymer_Battery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/NASA_Lithium_Ion_Polymer_Battery.jpg/220px-NASA_Lithium_Ion_Polymer_Battery.jpg" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/NASA_Lithium_Ion_Polymer_Battery.jpg/330px-NASA_Lithium_Ion_Polymer_Battery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/NASA_Lithium_Ion_Polymer_Battery.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_Lithium_Ion_Polymer_Battery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Prototypes of 75 watt-hour/kilogram <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_polymer_battery" title="Lithium-ion polymer battery" class="mw-redirect">lithium-ion polymer battery</a>. Newer lithium-ion cells can provide up to 130 W·h/kg and last through thousands of charging cycles.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_battery" title="Electric vehicle battery">Electric vehicle battery</a></div>
<p>Finding the economic balance of range against performance, <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a>, and accumulator type versus cost challenges every EV manufacturer.</p>
<p>While most current highway-speed electric vehicle designs focus on <a href="/wiki/Lithium_ion_battery" title="Lithium ion battery" class="mw-redirect">lithium-ion</a> and other lithium-based variants a variety of alternative batteries can also be used. Lithium based batteries are often chosen for their high power and energy density but have a limited shelf-life and cycle lifetime which can significantly increase the running costs of the vehicle. Variants such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium_iron_phosphate" title="Lithium iron phosphate">Lithium iron phosphate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithium-titanate_battery" title="Lithium-titanate battery" class="mw-redirect">Lithium-titanate</a> attempt to solve the durability issues with traditional lithium-ion batteries.</p>
<p>Other battery technologies include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lead_acid_batteries" title="Lead acid batteries" class="mw-redirect">Lead acid batteries</a> are still the most used form of power for most of the electric vehicles used today. The initial construction costs are significantly lower than for other battery types, and while power output to weight is poorer than other designs, range and power can be easily added by increasing the number of batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nickel-cadmium_battery" title="Nickel-cadmium battery" class="mw-redirect">NiCd</a> - Largely superseded by NiMH</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nickel_metal_hydride_battery" title="Nickel metal hydride battery" class="mw-redirect">Nickel metal hydride (NiMH)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nickel_iron_battery" title="Nickel iron battery" class="mw-redirect">Nickel iron battery</a> - Known for its comparatively long lifetime and low power density</li>
</ul>
<p>Several battery technologies are also in development such as:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zinc-air_battery" title="Zinc-air battery" class="mw-redirect">Zinc-air battery</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_battery" title="Molten salt battery">Molten salt battery</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zinc-bromine_flow_battery" title="Zinc-bromine flow battery" class="mw-redirect">Zinc-bromine flow batteries</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vanadium_redox_battery" title="Vanadium redox battery">Vanadium redox batteries</a> can be refilled, instead of recharged, saving time. The depleted electrolyte can be recharged at the point of exchange, or taken away to a remote station.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Travel_range_before_recharging">Travel range before recharging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Travel range before recharging">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Travel range before recharging" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The range of an electric car depends on the number and type of batteries used. The weight and type of vehicle, and the performance demands of the driver, also have an impact just as they do on the range of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_vehicle" title="Traditional vehicle" class="mw-redirect">traditional vehicles</a>. The range of an <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_conversion" title="Electric vehicle conversion">electric vehicle conversion</a> depends on the battery type:</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battery_swapping">Battery swapping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Battery swapping">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Battery swapping" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battery_swapping" title="Battery swapping" class="mw-redirect">Battery swapping</a></div>
<p>An alternative to quick recharging is to exchange a discharged battery or battery pack for a fully charged one, saving the delay of waiting for the vehicle's battery to charge. <a href="/wiki/Battery_swapping" title="Battery swapping" class="mw-redirect">Battery swapping</a> is common in warehouses using electric <a href="/wiki/Forklift_truck" title="Forklift truck">forklift trucks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> The concept of exchangeable battery service was first proposed as early as 1896 in order to overcome the limited operating range of electric cars and trucks.<sup id="cite_ref-HistEV_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistEV-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The concept was first put into practice by <a href="/wiki/Hartford_Electric_Light_Company" title="Hartford Electric Light Company" class="mw-redirect">Hartford Electric Light Company</a> through the GeVeCo battery service and was initially available for electric trucks. Both vehicles and batteries were modified to facilitate a fast battery exchange. The service was provided between 1910 to 1924 and during that period covered more than 6 million miles.<sup id="cite_ref-HistEV_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistEV-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> A rapid battery replacement system was implemented to keep running 50 electric buses at the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Better Place</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg/220px-Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg/330px-Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg/440px-Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Better_Place_Charging_Station_IMG_6670.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Better_Place" title="Better Place">Better Place</a>'s battery switching station in Israel</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Better_Place" title="Better Place">Better Place</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Better_Place" title="Better Place">Better Place</a> network was the first modern commercial deployment of the battery switching model. The <a href="/wiki/Renault_Fluence_Z.E." title="Renault Fluence Z.E.">Renault Fluence Z.E.</a> was the electric car developed with switchable battery technology for use in the Better Place network in operation in Israel and Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-BP01_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP01-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> The robotic battery-switching operation took five minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-JP240311_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JP240311-181"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> By late 2012 the company began to suffer financial difficulties, and decided to put on hold the roll out in Australia and reduce its non-core activities in North America, as the company decided to concentrate its resources on its two existing markets.<sup id="cite_ref-BP02_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP02-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After implementing the first modern commercial deployment of the battery swapping model in Israel and Denmark, Better Place filed for bankruptcy in Israel in May 2013. The company's financial difficulties were caused by the high investment required to develop the charging and swapping infrastructure, about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$850</span> million in private capital, and a market penetration significantly lower than originally expected by <a href="/wiki/Shai_Agassi" title="Shai Agassi">Shai Agassi</a>, who predicted that 100,000 electric cars would be on Israeli roads by 2010. Less than 1,000 Fluence Z.E. cars were deployed in Israel and around 400 units in Denmark.<sup id="cite_ref-BP03_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP03-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JPost052613_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JPost052613-186"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Under Better Place's business model, the company owns the batteries, so the court liquidator will have to decide what to do with customers who do not have ownership of the battery and risk being left with a useless car.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> In July 2013 Better Place was acquired by the Sunrise group which will pay <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Israeli_new_shekel" title="Israeli new shekel">₪</a>18 million</span> (<span style="white-space: nowrap">US$5 million</span>) for Better Place’s assets in Israel, and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Israeli_new_shekel" title="Israeli new shekel">₪</a>25 million</span> (<span style="white-space: nowrap">US$7 million</span>) for its intellectual property, held by Better Place Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg/220px-Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg" width="220" height="88" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg/330px-Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg/440px-Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tesla_charging_station_with_solar_collector_trimmed.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Panoramic view of <a href="/wiki/Tesla_supercharger" title="Tesla supercharger" class="mw-redirect">Tesla supercharger</a> rapid charging station in <a href="/wiki/Tejon,_California" title="Tejon, California">Tejon Ranch, California</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>Tesla Motors</dt>
</dl>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tesla_supercharger" title="Tesla supercharger" class="mw-redirect">Tesla supercharger</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a> designed its <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_S" title="Tesla Model S">Model S</a> to allow fast battery swapping.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> In June 2013, Tesla announced their goal to deploy a <a href="/wiki/Battery_swapping" title="Battery swapping" class="mw-redirect">battery swapping</a> station in each of its <a href="/wiki/Tesla_supercharger" title="Tesla supercharger" class="mw-redirect">supercharging stations</a>. At a demonstration event Tesla showed that a battery swap operation with the Model S takes just over 90 seconds, about half the time it takes to refill a gasoline-powered car.<sup id="cite_ref-Swapping01_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swapping01-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swapping02_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swapping02-191"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first stations are planned to be deployed along <a href="/wiki/Interstate_5_in_California" title="Interstate 5 in California">Interstate 5 in California</a> where, according to Tesla, a large number of Model S sedans make the San Francisco-Los Angeles trip regularly.
