These will be followed by the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_DC" title="Washington, DC" class="mw-redirect">Washington, DC</a> to <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> corridor. Each swapping station will cost <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$500,000</span> and will have about 50 batteries available without requiring reservations. The service would be offered for the price of about 15 US gallons (57&#160;l; 12&#160;imp&#160;gal) of gasoline at the current local rate, around <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$60</span> to <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$80</span> at June 2013 prices.<sup id="cite_ref-Swapping01_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swapping01-190"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vehicle-to-grid:_uploading_and_grid_buffering">Vehicle-to-grid: uploading and grid buffering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Vehicle-to-grid: uploading and grid buffering">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Vehicle-to-grid: uploading and grid buffering" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vehicle-to-grid" title="Vehicle-to-grid">Vehicle-to-grid</a></div>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economy_7" title="Economy 7">Economy 7</a>&#160;and <a href="/wiki/Load_balancing_(electrical_power)" title="Load balancing (electrical power)">load balancing (electrical power)</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">Smart grid</a> allows BEVs to provide power to the grid, specifically:</p>
<ul>
<li>During <a href="/wiki/Peak_load" title="Peak load" class="mw-redirect">peak load</a> periods, when the cost of electricity can be very high. These vehicles can then be recharged during <a href="/wiki/Off-peak" title="Off-peak" class="mw-redirect">off-peak</a> hours at cheaper rates while helping to absorb excess night time generation. Here the batteries in the vehicles serve as a distributed storage system to buffer power.</li>
<li>During <a href="/wiki/Power_outage" title="Power outage">blackouts</a>, as an emergency backup supply.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifespan">Lifespan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Lifespan">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Lifespan" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Battery life should be considered when calculating the extended cost of ownership, as all batteries eventually wear out and must be replaced. The rate at which they expire depends on the type of battery technology and how they are used&#160;— many types of batteries are damaged by depleting them beyond a certain level. Lithium-ion batteries degrade faster when stored at higher temperatures.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Future">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Future" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The future of battery electric vehicles depends primarily upon the cost and availability of <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">batteries</a> with high specific energy, power density, and long life, as all other aspects such as motors, motor controllers, and chargers are fairly mature and cost-competitive with internal combustion engine components. Diarmuid O'Connell, VP of Business Development at Tesla Motors, estimates that by the year 2020 30% of the cars driving on the road will be battery electric or plug-in hybrid.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Nissan CEO <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Ghosn" title="Carlos Ghosn">Carlos Ghosn</a> has predicted that one in 10 cars globally will run on battery power alone by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-prediction_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prediction-193"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally a recent report claims that by 2020 electric cars and other <a href="/wiki/Green_vehicle" title="Green vehicle">green cars</a> will take a third of the total of global car sales.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It is estimated that there are sufficient lithium reserves to power 4 billion electric cars.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_methods_of_energy_storage">Other methods of energy storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other methods of energy storage">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other methods of energy storage" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Experimental <a href="/wiki/Electric_double-layer_capacitor" title="Electric double-layer capacitor">supercapacitors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flywheel_energy_storage" title="Flywheel energy storage">flywheel energy storage</a> devices offer comparable storage capacity, faster charging, and lower volatility. They have the potential to overtake batteries as the preferred rechargeable storage for EVs.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_Internationale_de_l%27Automobile" title="Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile">FIA</a> included their use in its sporting regulations of energy systems for <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> race vehicles in 2007 (for supercapacitors) and 2009 (for flywheel energy storage devices).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_cars">Solar cars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Solar cars">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Solar cars" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_vehicle" title="Solar vehicle">Solar vehicle</a></div>
<p>Solar cars are electric vehicles powered completely or significantly by direct <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a>, usually, through <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect">photovoltaic</a> (PV) cells contained in <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> that convert the sun's energy directly into electric energy.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charging">Charging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Charging">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Charging" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eletroposto6.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Eletroposto6.jpg/220px-Eletroposto6.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Eletroposto6.jpg/330px-Eletroposto6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Eletroposto6.jpg/440px-Eletroposto6.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eletroposto6.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Charging station at <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>. This station is run by <a href="/wiki/Petrobras" title="Petrobras">Petrobras</a> and uses <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Charging_station" title="Charging station">charging station</a></div>
<p>Batteries in BEVs must be periodically recharged (see also Replacing, above).</p>
<p>Unlike vehicles powered by fossil fuels, BEVs are most commonly and conveniently charged from the <a href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid" class="mw-redirect">power grid</a> overnight at home, without the inconvenience of having to go to a filling station. Charging can also be done using a street or shop charging station.</p>
<p>The electricity on the grid is in turn generated from a variety of sources; such as <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_power_plant" title="Fossil fuel power plant" class="mw-redirect">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a> and others. Power sources such as roof top <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect">photovoltaic</a> solar cell panels, <a href="/wiki/Micro_hydro" title="Micro hydro">micro hydro</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a> may also be used and are promoted because of concerns regarding <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.</p>
<p>As part of its commitment to environmental sustainability, the Dutch government initiated a plan to establish over 200 recharging stations for electric vehicles across the country by 2015. The rollout will be undertaken by Switzerland-based power and automation company ABB and Dutch startup Fastned, and will aim to provide at least one station every 50 kilometres (31 miles) for the Netherlands' 16 million residents.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Reports emerged in late July 2013 of a significant conflict between the companies responsible for the two types of charging machines. The Japanese-developed CHAdeMO standard is favored by Nissan, Mitsubishi, and Toyota, while the Society of Automotive Engineers’ (SAE) International J1772 Combo standard is backed by GM, Ford, Volkswagen, and BMW. Both are direct-current quick-charging systems designed to charge the battery of an electric vehicle to 80 percent in approximately 20 minutes, but the two systems are completely incompatible. In light of an ongoing feud between the two companies, experts in the field warned that the momentum of the electric vehicle market will be severely affected.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Julia_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Julia-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Richard Martin, editorial director for clean technology marketing and consultant firm Navigant Research, stated:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Fast charging, however and whenever it gets built out, is going to be key for the development of a mainstream market for plug-in electric vehicles. The broader conflict between the CHAdeMO and SAE Combo connectors, we see that as a hindrance to the market over the next several years that needs to be worked out.<sup id="cite_ref-Julia_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Julia-201"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_Charging_Standards">US Charging Standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: US Charging Standards">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: US Charging Standards" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Around 1998 the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a> classified levels of charging power that have been codified in title 13 of the California Code of Regulations, the U.S. 1999 <a href="/wiki/National_Electrical_Code" title="National Electrical Code">National Electrical Code</a> section 625 and <a href="/wiki/SAE_International" title="SAE International">SAE International</a> standards.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Three standards were developed, termed Level 1, Level 2, and Level 3 charging.</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Level</th>
<th>Original definition<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup></th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Coulomb_Technologies" title="Coulomb Technologies">Coulomb Technologies</a>' definition<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup></th>
<th>Connectors</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Level 1</th>
<td>AC energy to the vehicle's on-board charger; from the most common U.S. grounded household receptacle, commonly referred to as a 120 volt outlet.</td>
<td>120&#160;V&#160;AC; 16&#160;A (= 1.92&#160;kW)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/SAE_J1772" title="SAE J1772">SAE J1772</a> (16.8&#160;kW),<br />
<a href="/wiki/NEMA_connector" title="NEMA connector">NEMA 5-15</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Level 2</th>
<td>AC energy to the vehicle's on-board charger; 208 - 240 volt, <a href="/wiki/Single-phase_electric_power" title="Single-phase electric power">single phase</a>. The maximum current specified is 32 amps (continuous) with a branch circuit breaker rated at 40 amps. Maximum continuous input power is specified as 7.68&#160;kW (= 240V x 32A*).</td>
<td>208-240&#160;V&#160;AC;<br />
12&#160;A - 80&#160;A (= 2.5 - 19.2&#160;kW)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/SAE_J1772" title="SAE J1772">SAE J1772</a> (16.8&#160;kW),<br />
<a href="/wiki/IEC_62196" title="IEC 62196">IEC 62196</a> (44&#160;kW),<br />
<a href="/wiki/Magne_Charge" title="Magne Charge">Magne&#160;Charge (Obsolete)</a>,<br />
<a href="/wiki/Avcon" title="Avcon">Avcon</a>,<br />
<a href="/wiki/IEC_60309" title="IEC 60309">IEC&#160;60309&#160;16&#160;A</a>&#160;(3.8&#160;kW)<br />
IEC 62198-2 Type2 same as VDE-AR-E 2623-2-2, also known as the Mennekes connector (43.5&#160;kW)IEC 62198-2 Type3 also known as Scame</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Level 3</th>
<td>DC energy from an off-board charger; there is no minimum energy requirement but the maximum current specified is 400 amps and 240&#160;kW continuous power supplied.</td>
<td>very high voltages (300-600&#160;V&#160;DC); very high currents (hundreds of Amperes)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Magne_Charge" title="Magne Charge">Magne&#160;Charge (Obsolete)</a><br />
<a href="/wiki/CH%CE%9BdeMO" title="CHΛdeMO" class="mw-redirect">CHΛdeMO</a> (62.5&#160;kW), <a href="/wiki/SAE_J1772" title="SAE J1772">SAE J1772</a> Combo, <a href="/wiki/IEC_62196" title="IEC 62196">IEC 62196</a> Mennekes Combo</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>.* or potentially 208V x 37&#160;A, out of the strict specification but within circuit breaker and connector/cable power limits. Alternatively, this voltage would impose a lower power rating of 6.7&#160;kW at 32&#160;A.</p>
<p>More recently the term "Level 3" has also been used by the SAE J1772 Standard Committee for a possible future higher-power AC fast charging standard.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> To distinguish from Level 3 DC fast charging, this would-be standard is written as "Level 3 AC". SAE has not yet approved standards for either AC or DC Level 3 charging.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As of June 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, some electric cars provide charging options that do not fit within the older California "Level 1, 2, and 3 charging" standard, with its top charging rate of 40&#160;Amps. For example, the Tesla Roadster may be charged at a rate up to 70&#160;Amps (16.8&#160;kW) with a wall-mounted charger.<sup id="cite_ref-tm20120610_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tm20120610-206"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>For comparison in Europe the IEC 61851-1 charging modes are used to classify charging equipment. The provisions of <a href="/wiki/IEC_62196#Charging_modes" title="IEC 62196">IEC 62196 charging modes</a> for conductive charging of electric vehicles include Mode 1 (max. 16&#160;A / max. 250&#160;V a.c. or 480&#160;V three-phase), Mode 2 (max. 32&#160;A / max. 250&#160;V a.c. or 480&#160;V three-phase), Mode 3 (max. 63A (70A U.S.) / max. 690&#160;V a.c. or three-phase) and Mode 4 (max. 400&#160;A / max. 600&#160;V d.c.).<sup id="cite_ref-table1_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-table1-207"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Connectors">Connectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Connectors">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Connectors" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most electric cars have used <a href="/wiki/Conductive_coupling" title="Conductive coupling">conductive coupling</a> to supply electricity for recharging after the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a> settled on the <a href="/wiki/SAE_J1772" title="SAE J1772">SAE J1772</a>-2001 standard<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> as the charging interface for electric vehicles in California in June 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> In Europe the <a href="/wiki/European_Automobile_Manufacturers_Association" title="European Automobile Manufacturers Association">ACEA</a> has decided to use the Type 2 connector from the range of <a href="/wiki/IEC_62196#Plug_types" title="IEC 62196">IEC_62196 plug types</a> for conductive charging of electric vehicles in the European Union as the Type 1 connector (SAE J1772-2009) does not provide for three-phase charging.<sup id="cite_ref-acea2010_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acea2010-210"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Another approach is <a href="/wiki/Inductive_charging#Electric_vehicles" title="Inductive charging">inductive charging</a> using a non-conducting "paddle" inserted into a slot in the car. <a href="/wiki/Delco_Electronics" title="Delco Electronics">Delco Electronics</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Magne_Charge" title="Magne Charge">Magne Charge</a> inductive charging system around 1998 for the <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_EV1" title="General Motors EV1">General Motors EV1</a> and it was also used for the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_S-10_EV" title="Chevrolet S-10 EV">Chevrolet S-10 EV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toyota_RAV4_EV" title="Toyota RAV4 EV">Toyota RAV4 EV</a> vehicles.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regenerative_braking">Regenerative braking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Regenerative braking">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Regenerative braking" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking" class="mw-redirect">Regenerative braking</a></div>
<p>Using <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking" class="mw-redirect">regenerative braking</a>, a feature which is present on many <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">hybrid electric vehicles</a>, approximately 20% of the energy usually lost in the brakes is recovered to recharge the batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-TwoBillion_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwoBillion-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charging_time">Charging time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Charging time">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Charging time" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg/220px-Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg/330px-Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg/440px-Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Smart_ED_charging_WAS_2011_1123.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Smart_ED" title="Smart ED" class="mw-redirect">Smart ED</a> charging from a Level 2 station</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>More electrical power to the car reduces charging time. Power is limited by the capacity of the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">grid</a> connection, and, for level 1 and 2 charging, by the power rating of the car's on-board charger. A normal <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_outlet" title="Electrical outlet" class="mw-redirect">outlet</a> is between 1.5&#160;kW (in the US, Canada, Japan, and other countries with 110 <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">volt</a> supply) to 3&#160;kW (in countries with 230V supply). The main connection to a house may sustain 10, 15 or even 20&#160;kW in addition to "normal" domestic loads—although, it would be unwise to use all the apparent capability—and special wiring can be installed to use this.</p>
<p>As examples of on-board chargers, the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> at launch has a 3.3&#160;kW charger<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a> can accept up to 16.8&#160;kW (240V at 70A) from the <i>High Power Wall Connector</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-tm20120610_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tm20120610-206"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> These power numbers are small compared to the effective power delivery rate of an average <a href="/wiki/Filling_station" title="Filling station">petrol pump</a>, about 5,000&#160;kW.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Even if the electrical supply power can be increased, most batteries do not accept charge at greater than their <a href="/wiki/Battery_charger#Charge_rate" title="Battery charger">charge rate</a> ("1<i>C</i>"), because high charge rates have an adverse effect on the discharge capacities of batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite these power limitations, plugging in to even the least-powerful conventional home outlet provides more than 15 <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour" class="mw-redirect">kilowatt-hours</a> of energy overnight, sufficient to propel most electric cars more than 70 kilometres (43&#160;mi) (see <a href="#Energy_efficiency">Energy efficiency</a> above).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hobbyists.2C_conversions.2C_and_racing">Hobbyists, conversions, and racing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Hobbyists, conversions, and racing">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Hobbyists, conversions, and racing" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eliica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Eliica.jpg/220px-Eliica.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Eliica.jpg/330px-Eliica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Eliica.jpg/440px-Eliica.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eliica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eliica" title="Eliica">Eliica</a> prototype</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:URE05e.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/URE05e.jpg/220px-URE05e.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/URE05e.jpg/330px-URE05e.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/URE05e.jpg/440px-URE05e.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:URE05e.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The full electric <a href="/wiki/Formula_Student" title="Formula Student">Formula Student</a> car of the <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven_University_of_Technology" title="Eindhoven University of Technology">Eindhoven University of Technology</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hobbyists often build their own EVs by <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_conversion" title="Electric vehicle conversion">converting</a> existing production cars to run solely on electricity. There is a <a href="/wiki/Cottage_industry" title="Cottage industry" class="mw-redirect">cottage industry</a> supporting the conversion and construction of BEVs by hobbyists.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> Universities such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Irvine" title="University of California, Irvine">University of California, Irvine</a> even build their own custom electric or hybrid-electric cars from scratch.</p>
<p>Short-range battery electric vehicles can offer the hobbyist comfort, utility, and quickness, sacrificing only range. Short-range EVs may be built using high-performance lead–acid batteries, using about half the mass needed for a 100 to 130 km (60 to 80 mi) range. The result is a vehicle with about a 50&#160;km (30&#160;mi) range, which, when designed with appropriate weight distribution (40/60 front to rear), does not require <a href="/wiki/Power_steering" title="Power steering">power steering</a>, offers exceptional acceleration in the lower end of its operating range, and is freeway capable and legal. But their EVs are expensive due to the higher cost for these higher-performance batteries. By including a <a href="/wiki/Manual_transmission" title="Manual transmission">manual transmission</a>, short-range EVs can obtain both better performance and greater efficiency than the single-speed EVs developed by major manufacturers. Unlike the converted golf carts used for <a href="/wiki/Neighborhood_electric_vehicle" title="Neighborhood electric vehicle" class="mw-redirect">neighborhood electric vehicles</a>, short-range EVs may be operated on typical suburban throughways (where 60–80&#160;km/h / 35-50&#160;mph speed limits are typical) and can keep up with traffic typical on such roads and the short "slow-lane" on-and-off segments of freeways common in suburban areas.</p>
<p>Faced with chronic fuel shortage on the <a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">Gaza Strip</a>, Palestinian electrical engineer Waseem Othman al-Khozendar invented in 2008 a way to convert his car to run on 32 electric batteries. According to al-Khozendar, the batteries can be charged with <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$2</span> worth of electricity to drive from 180 to 240 km (110 to 150 mi). After a 7-hour charge, the car should also be able to run up to a speed of 100&#160;km/h (60&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Japanese Professor Hiroshi Shimizu from Faculty of Environmental Information of the <a href="/wiki/Keio_University" title="Keio University">Keio University</a> created an electric limousine: the <a href="/wiki/Eliica" title="Eliica">Eliica</a> (Electric Lithium-Ion Car) has eight wheels with electric 55&#160;kW hub motors (8WD) with an output of 470&#160;kW and zero emissions, a top speed of 370&#160;km/h (230&#160;mph), and a maximum range of 320&#160;km (200&#160;mi) provided by <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion batteries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> However, current models cost approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$300,000</span>, about one third of which is the cost of the batteries.</p>
<p>In 2008, several Chinese manufacturers began marketing <a href="/wiki/Lithium_iron_phosphate_battery" title="Lithium iron phosphate battery">lithium iron phosphate (<span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">LiFePO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
4</span></span>) batteries</a> directly to hobbyists and vehicle conversion shops. These batteries offered much better power-to-weight ratios allowing vehicle conversions to typically achieve 75 to 150 mi (120 to 240 km) per charge. Prices gradually declined to approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$350</span> per kW·h by mid-2009. As the <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">LiFePO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
4</span></span> cells feature life ratings of 3,000 cycles, compared to typical lead acid battery ratings of 300 cycles, the life expectancy of <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">LiFePO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
4</span></span> cells is around 10&#160;years. This has led to a resurgence in the number of vehicles converted by individuals. <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">LiFePO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />
4</span></span> cells do require more expensive battery management and charging systems than lead acid batteries.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Electric_drag_racing" title="Electric drag racing">Electric drag racing</a> is a sport where electric vehicles start from standstill and attempt the highest possible speed over a short given distance.<sup id="cite_ref-0EX1_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-0EX1-217"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> They sometimes race and usually beat gasoline sports cars.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Organizations such as <a href="/wiki/NEDRA" title="NEDRA" class="mw-redirect">NEDRA</a> keep track of records world wide using certified equipment.</p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Formula_Student" title="Formula Student">Formula Student</a> competition at the <a href="/wiki/Silverstone_Circuit" title="Silverstone Circuit">Silverstone Circuit</a> in July 2013, the electric powered car of the <a href="/wiki/ETH_Zurich" title="ETH Zurich">ETH Zurich</a> won against all cars with internal combustion engines. It is believed to be the first time that an electric vehicle has beaten cars powered by combustion engines in any accredited motorsport competition.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Currently_available_electric_cars">Currently available electric cars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Currently available electric cars">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Currently available electric cars" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Currently_available_electric_cars" title="Currently available electric cars" class="mw-redirect">Currently available electric cars</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Highway_capable">Highway capable</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Highway capable">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Highway capable" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_production_battery_electric_vehicles" title="List of production battery electric vehicles">List of production battery electric vehicles</a></div>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">Plug-in electric vehicle</a>&#160;and <a href="/wiki/List_of_modern_production_plug-in_electric_vehicles" title="List of modern production plug-in electric vehicles">list of modern production plug-in electric vehicles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg/220px-Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg/330px-Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg/440px-Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Google_Electric_Car_Seciurity_2008_2592452314_780b7871e1_o.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Global_Electric_Motorcars" title="Global Electric Motorcars">GEM</a> is the world's top selling <a href="/wiki/Neighborhood_electric_vehicle" title="Neighborhood electric vehicle" class="mw-redirect">neighborhood electric vehicle</a>, with more than 46,000 units sold by April 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-GEMsales_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GEMsales-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of June 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the number of <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a> highway-capable all-electric passenger cars available in the market is limited to about 20 models. Most electric vehicles in the world roads are low-speed, low-range <a href="/wiki/Neighborhood_electric_vehicle" title="Neighborhood electric vehicle" class="mw-redirect">neighborhood electric vehicles</a> (NEVs) or electric <a href="/wiki/Heavy_quadricycle" title="Heavy quadricycle" class="mw-redirect">quadricycles</a>. Pike Research estimated there were almost 479,000 NEVs on the world roads in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-NEVsales_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEVsales-220"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> As of November 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the top selling NEV is the <a href="/wiki/Global_Electric_Motorcars" title="Global Electric Motorcars">Global Electric Motorcars</a> (GEM) family of vehicles, with global sales of more than 46,000 units since 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-GEMsales_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GEMsales-219"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> The two largest NEV markets in 2011 were the United States, with 14,737 units sold, and France, with 2,231 units.<sup id="cite_ref-PikeR_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PikeR-221"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Renault_Twizy" title="Renault Twizy">Renault Twizy</a> all-electric <a href="/wiki/Heavy_quadricycle" title="Heavy quadricycle" class="mw-redirect">heavy quadricycle</a>, launched in Europe in March 2012 and with global sales of 9,020 units through December 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-TwizySales_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwizySales-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> became the best selling <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-in electric vehicle</a> in Europe for 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-TopPEV2012_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TopPEV2012-223"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> The top selling markets were Germany with 2,413 units, France with 2,232 units, and Italy with 1,545 units sold in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-TwizySales_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TwizySales-222"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg/220px-Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg/330px-Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg/440px-Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nissan_LEAF_got_thirsty_trimmed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> is the world's best selling highway-capable all-electric car in history, with global sales of over 75,000 units sold by August 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaf75K_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaf75K-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of June 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_car&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, more than 150,000 highway-capable all-electric passenger cars and light utility vehicles have been sold worldwide since 2008. By mid July 2013 the leading electric vehicle manufacturer is the <a href="/wiki/Renault-Nissan_Alliance" title="Renault-Nissan Alliance" class="mw-redirect">Renault-Nissan Alliance</a> with global sales of 100,000 all-electric vehicles since December 2010, which includes over 71,000 <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leafs</a>, about 11,000 <a href="/wiki/Renault_Twizy" title="Renault Twizy">Renault Twizy</a> <a href="/wiki/Heavy_quadricycle" title="Heavy quadricycle" class="mw-redirect">heavy quadricycles</a>, almost 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Renault_Kangoo_Z.E." title="Renault Kangoo Z.E." class="mw-redirect">Renault Kangoo Z.E.</a> utility vans, about 5,000 <a href="/wiki/Renault_Zoe" title="Renault Zoe">Renault Zoes</a>, and over 3,000 <a href="/wiki/Renault_Fluence_Z.E." title="Renault Fluence Z.E.">Renault Fluence Z.E.</a> electric cars.<sup id="cite_ref-RN100K_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RN100K-224"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SalesRnlt2011_13_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalesRnlt2011_13-225"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> Ranking second is <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Motors" title="Mitsubishi Motors">Mitsubishi Motors</a> with global sales of over 30,000 all-electric vehicles between July 2009 and June 2013, and its all-electric line up includes the <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_i-MiEV" title="Mitsubishi i-MiEV">Mitsubishi i-MiEV</a>, the rebadged <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_iOn" title="Peugeot iOn" class="mw-redirect">Peugeot iOn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Citro%C3%ABn_C-Zero" title="Citroën C-Zero" class="mw-redirect">Citroën C-Zero</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Minicab_MiEV" title="Mitsubishi Minicab MiEV" class="mw-redirect">Mitsubishi Minicab MiEV</a> utility van and truck.<sup id="cite_ref-iMiEV30K_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iMiEV30K-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JapMiEV09_13_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JapMiEV09_13-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a> is the third best selling all-electric vehicle manufacturer, with more than 15,000 electric cars sold since February 2008, including almost 2,500 <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster" title="Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadsters</a> and 12,700 <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_S" title="Tesla Model S">Tesla Model S</a> sold through June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Tesla4Q2012_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tesla4Q2012-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tesla1Q2013_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tesla1Q2013-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tesla2Q2013_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tesla2Q2013-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
